Volume 28, Number 3 May •  June 2024 CONTENTS Next-generation data centers supporting high-growth artificial intelligence (AI) applications require massive new scale in high-performance computing (HPC) and high-bandwidth memory (HBM), as well as higher energy efficiency. Multicolumn electron-beam lithography delivers high resolution, large field of view, huge depth of focus, and adaptable patterning capabilities that enable patterning of very high-density, low-power interconnects that deliver the speed and power required to fuel next-generation chip performance in high-growth AI-driven applications. Chiplets: The mix and match design revolution By Adrienne Downey  [TechInsights Inc.] C TECHNOLOGY TRENDS has been a way to protect the chips and enable electr ical connections to other devices. Solutions like multi-chip modules (MCMs) that br ing several chips together in one package have been around for decades, but they were of limited use because the technology was never perfected. It was also expensive and resulted in a lot of y ield loss. Advanced packaging came along to improve system performance compared to traditional packages and deal with size constraints. Wafer-level packaging emerged in 2000 and then 2.5D around 2010. When through-silicon-vias (TSVs) were perfected, 3D stacking became the next step in advanced packaging in the 20-teens. Now, chiplets have emerged, enabled by the advances in packaging technologies. Chiplets are a disrupt ive inf luence in the market and are changing the way we design products. The concept behind chiplets is to take a large SoC design and divide it into subsets of functionality, where each chiplet is optimized for a particular function, like a central processing unit (CPU) core or a memory controller. Some people like to compare this to building with LEGO® bricks. Chiplets are known good die that are interconnected in a package, either side by side, stacked, or both. By hiplets have become one of the hottest topics in the semiconductor industry for good reason. They take the concept of a single, large, highly-integrated system on a chip (SoC) and break it up into blocks of functionality called chiplets. With interconnect standards and a strong ecosystem, designers will eventually be able to pick chiplets designed by different companies, built at their optimized technology nodes, add in their own hardware and software expertise, and create new designs not possible today. Chiplet revenues will grow to $236 billion by 2030 at a compound annual growth rate (CAGR) of 19.7%. Unit shipments for central processing units/graphics processing units (CPUs/GPUs) chiplets in the computing segment will grow at a 27.3%, with RISC-V making up a growing percentage of the total. How it all began I n  19 91,  D r.  Ts u g io  M a k i m o t o published a paper about an observation he made regarding the semiconductor industry and how it alternates between standardization and customization every ten years (Figure 1). This observation is refer red to as Makimoto’s Wave, and it described the industry’s cycles beginning in 1957 with standard discrete devices to the rise of application-specific integrated circuits (ASICs) between 1987-1997. The next decade resulted in field-programmable devices, followed by SoC/system in package (SiP) devices. The current decade from 2017-2027, h ighl ighted by the development of highly-f lexible integration and enabled by the rise of chiplets, is the fourth swing to standardization in Makimoto’s Wave. Even more prescient was Gordon E. Moore’s observat ion in his 1965 paper, “Cramming Components Onto Integ rated Ci rcu it s”  [2],  i n  wh ich he stated, “It may prove to be more economical to build large systems out of smaller functions, which are separately packaged and interconnected.” Software costs are skyrocketing, design times are lengthening, and yields are shrinking with the large die SoCs that became popular during the last “customization” portion of Makimoto’s Wave. In both cases, these leaders of the semiconductor industry foresaw the eventual need to move away from large, h ighly-integrated chips to smaller, more specialized devices connected together. This concept was enabled by the combination of two technologies: chiplets and leading-edge packaging. Traditional semiconductor packaging Figure 1: Extended Makimoto’s Wave. SOURCE: [1]
themselves, chiplets are not a packaging methodology—rather, they represent a design strategy. In fact ,  there are many dif ferent package architectures available for ch iplet s ,  depend ing on the power, performance, area, and cost requirements of the end product. Newer packaging technologies include 2.5D, 3D, chip-on -wafe r- on - subs t r a t e  (CoWoS ®) , embedded multi-die interconnect bridge (EMI B),  Si l icon Wafer  I nteg rated Fan-out Technology (SWIFT®), and many more, with the goal of keeping interconnect costs as low as possible while maximizing yield. It is useful to compare the pros and cons of chiplets vs. monolithic (highly- integrated) solutions. Chiplets have many benefits; one of the most prominent is that they can be mixed-and-matched with chiplets from different chipmakers and from different process nodes. This means they can be individually optimized for a particular function. In addition, the same chiplet can be reused in a wide variety of applications, and different design teams can be working on different chiplets in parallel, which lowers time to market of the overall solution. Yields are higher because defective chiplets can be swapped out, rather than having to swap out an entire monolithic chip; this makes chiplets more cost effective as well. However, one of the problems with chiplets is that the interconnect becomes a crucial and costly part of the design. Another is the issue of thermals, especially in stacked implementations. On the other hand, monolithic SoCs enable very tight integration for a solution with superior performance. These chips have lower latency because the features are physical ly closer together than with chiplets. While they are ideal for high-volume and higher performance applications, they ultimately have longer development cycles with lower yields at a higher cost. The ecosystem needs work T h e  c h i p l e t  e c o s y s t e m  i s  s t i l l developing. The “big three” electronic design automation (EDA) companies all support chiplet design, but there is more work to be done given that the integration of the different chiplets along with interconnects increases the design complexity, which, in turn, complicates design verification. On the foundry side, TSMC and Intel are big players in this market; both companies offer several proprietary packaging and interconnect technologies. The major outsourced semiconductor and test suppliers (OSATs)—including Amkor, ASE and JCET—are heavily involved in the chiplet ecosystem. IBM has a facility in Bromont, Canada that makes 100,000 advanced flip-chip modules each week, and they have been publishing chiplet research for decades. AMD has been at the forefront of chiplet designs beginning with EPYC in 2017 that had four chiplets; they now have chiplets in several different product lines. The Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency (DARPA) has been working on chiplets since 2017 with its Common Heterogeneous Integration and IP Reuse Strategies (CHIPS) program. It is working with a number of companies, including GE, Intel, Keysight, Northrop Grumman, Qorvo and Xilinx to establish heterogeneous packaging facilities in the U.S. that can support the Department of Defense (DoD). One challenge with chiplet production is that the test companies will see an increased workload due to the higher vo l u m e s  of  i n d iv id u a l  d i e  b e i n g produced. Additionally, they will need to make sure the die is good before sealing it in a package underneath other chiplets in a stacked design. The importance of interconnects A n o t h e r  c h a l l e n g e  f a c i n g  t h e developing ecosystem is the need for standards. Interconnects are a crucial piece of the puzzle for maximizing performance, and a number of efforts are underway to capture the industry’s attention. The Open Compute Project’s Open Domain-Specif ic Architecture projec t  i s  developing a  d ie - to - d ie i n t e rcon nec t  s t a nd a rd ,  a s  wel l  a s a  ch iple t  de s ig n  excha nge  a c ros s suppliers. Recently, Arm announced it is working with more than twenty partners to develop the Arm Chiplet S y s t e m  A r c h i t e c t u r e  t o  s u p p o r t more intellectual property (IP) reuse among suppliers. Arm also committed to extend ing the A MBA CHI C2C specification to include chiplets. The Universal Chiplet Interconnect Express™ (UCIe™) supports standard and advanced packaging: 2D, 2.5D and bridge packages with 3D suppor t in the future. It is meant to work with a variety of bridges like EMIB, Foveros, fanout, or interposers to al low one UCIe™ chiplet to talk to another. It includes a complete stack for the die-to-die interface rather than focusing only on specific layers. UCIe™ enjoys broad indust ry suppor t: the UCIe™ group includes 12 Board members, 80 Contributor members, and 30 Adopter members, representing a large swath of the indust r y. UCIe™ appears to be gain ing t ract ion, just l ike VCR technology triumphed over Betamax Figure 2: Chiplets vs. monolithic revenues. SOURCE: TechInsights Inc.
technology back in the dark ages of recorded video. Intel donated the initial specification for UCIe™ to jumpstart it and make it an open industry standard interconnect. UCIe™ 1.1 was released last August. Intel has demonstrated working UCIe™-enabled silicon with Pike Creek, and the company plans to use UCIe™ on the A r row Lake consumer processors. O t he r  i n t e r faces  i n  u se  i nclude OCP’s Bunch of Wires (BoW), the A d v a n c e d  I n t e r f a c e  B u s  (A I B) , Nv id i a’s  N V Li n k ®,  a nd  El iya n’s NuLink™. The more that interconnect choices can consol idate ,  the more likely that the mix-and-match future t h a t  c h i p l e t s  p r o m i s e  w i l l  c o m e to fruition. Sizing up the market F i g u r e  2  s h o w s  t o t a l  c h i p l e t r evenues  compa red  to  monol i t h ic devices over time. The chiplet market is expected to grow from 15% of the total chip market to a third by 2030. This equates to an increase from $67 bi l l ion in 2023 to $236 bi l l ion by 2030 at a CAGR of 19.7%, compared to a CAGR of 3.8% for the monolithic portion of the market. Most of the activity in the chiplet market is in the high-end computing and communications markets (Figure 3 ) .  S e r v e r s ,  h i g h - p e r f o r m a n c e computing (HPC), and data centers are all able to benefit from the move to chiplets, in part due to the growth in AI. In consumer personal computer (PC) gaming, the use of chiplets has brought faster  per for mance.  AMD demonstrated a 15% improvement in gaming performance with 3D-stacked L3 cache in the Ryzen 7 5800X3D d e s k t o p  C P U .  C e r t a i n  e d g e -computing markets require specif ic use cases, such as advanced dr iver assistance systems (ADAS), which can take advantage of the f lexibility offered by chiplets. The chiplet market can be divided by microarchitecture: x86, Arm, and RISC-V. Figures 4  and 5  focus on CPUs and GPUs in the comput ing seg ment .  Un it s  w i l l  i nc rea se  a t  a CAGR of 27.3% from 84 million in 2023 to 455 million by 2030. The x86 Figure 3: Chiplet units by application. SOURCE: TechInsights Inc. microarchitecture will make up one-third of all unit shipments in 2024, but its share will fall to 26% by 2030. Arm accounts for two-thirds of the computing chiplet market, but Arm’s share will be impacted by the rise of the RISC-V microarchitecture, which will grow to 25% of the total by 2030. The gray line indicates the desktop PC, notebook PC, and tablet portion of the computing chiplet market, which accounts for about 36% of the total in 2024. Meanwhile, the orange line— represent ing servers, workstat ions, and dat a  cente r  sol id-s t a te  d r ives (SSDs) — shows a  CAGR of  28.2% while maintaining lower-60% of the total computing chiplet market share. The x86 architecture will retain the top spot  t h rough 2025,  wh i le  R ISC-V will make up about 5% of the total by 2030. The server/workstation portion of the market makes up an average of 85-90% of the total. Summary Ultimately, chiplets and advanced pa ck ag i ng  w i l l  he lp  i nc r e a se  t he transistor count past what is achievable with the ret icle l imit of single-die implementations. As chiplet technology matures, it will become off-the-shelf, plug-and-play type functions, enabling designers to put their own spin on their designs. Broad adopt ion of UCIe™ across the chiplet ecosystem will help enable this vision of “mix-and-match” modular design capability. References 1. T. Makimoto, “Implicat ions of Makimoto’s Wave,” IEEE Computer, Vol. 46, pp. 32-37, 2013;  https:// www.shmj.or.jp/makimoto/pdf / makimoto_04_01.pdf 2. G. This is a challenge for chipmakers as the upcoming generation of high-NA EUV scanners a re expected to further reduce this field size by half, to 16.5mm × 26mm. This shrinking field size constrains the area of each chip and limits the number of transistors in a given chip. Conventional scaling of transistors in accordance with Moore’s Law has also slowed, while the cost to design and manufacture leading-edge chips continues to rise (Figure 1) [2-3]. It is increasingly difficult for chipmakers to deliver required performance and yield at reasonable costs (Figure 2) [1]. At the same time, more chip-chip interconnects are needed to support the growing demand for data processing [4]. Continued scaling will now require tighter integration of multiple chips with increasing numbers of chip-to-chip interconnects. Fortunately, high-resolution adaptable patterning with MEBL combined with new integration technologies enables such scaling [5]. C h i p l e t - b a s e d  d e s i g n s  e n a b l e cont inued scaling and performance i mprovement s ,  a nd prov ide  space a nd  p owe r  r e d u c t ion  b e nef i t s  by employing denser interconnects. This means breaking complex chips into smaller chips called “chiplets” to avoid limitations imposed by today’s chip Semiconductor scaling with high-productivity multicolumn electron-beam lithography By David K. Lam, Kenneth P. MacWilliams  [Multibeam Corporation] usinesses and consumers a r e  a d o p t i n g  a r t i f i c i a l intelligence (AI) at a rapid pace, placing enormous new demands on computing and communications. Next-generation data centers supporting upcoming AI applications will require massive new scale in high-performance computing (HPC) and high- bandwidth memor y (HBM), as wel l  as h igher energy efficiency. The industry faces major challenges in meeting this surge in demand. While the number of transistors has grown to 100 billion or more in today’s state-of-the-art semiconductor chips, new AI applications have a nea r insat iable demand for additional computing performance. As the cost and complexity of continued scaling are increasing rapidly, leading chipmakers are turning to advanced integration of multiple chips to deliver necessary performance improvements. Advanced chip integration is gaining adoption, but is also facing challenges: chip-to-chip communication bandwidth, I /O density, energy per bit t ransfer, and thermal effects. These must be addressed to reach performance levels required by AI, high-performance and edge computing, 5G/6G infrastructure, high-speed communications, and other emerging applications. B This paper shows how our multicolumn e-beam lithography (MEBL) system, working with advanced integrat ion technologies, can help address these challenges and achieve the next-level of performance—in computing, bandwidth, and power efficiency. Challenges in continued scaling Major challenges facing chipmakers in continued scaling are not easily resolved. Some of these challenges are: Silicon area scaling limit of one “classic” reticle (26mm X 33mm), being further reduced to a half-size reticle in high-numerical aperture (NA) extreme ultraviolet (EUV) lithography systems; High cost and slowing progress in feature size scaling; Energy required to move bits from one ch ip to another increasing rapidly, consuming more than 70% of total power of the chip in some cases; and Cost and time of new full designs exceeding half a billion dollars [1] with long timelines. Advanced chip designs have reached the maximum size of a single scanner f ield ,  cu r rent ly at  33mm × 26mm. Figure 1: Slowdown of silicon scaling as highlighted in presentations by: a) NVIDA [2]; and b) ASML [3]. SOURCES: NVIDIA, ASML, respectively
size constraints. By integrating these chiplets into multi-chiplet packages, manufacturers gain improved flexibility, scalability, and efficiency, allowing them to customize chiplet combinations for specific functions or applications. To meet new demands for high-density chip-to-chip bandwidth and power efficiency, advanced mult i - ch iple t  packag ing will turn to high-resolution adaptive patterning offered by the MEBL system. L e a d i ng  ch ipm a ke r s  h ave  b e e n shifting towards heterogeneous multi-chip modules (MCMs)—away from the monolithic systems-on-chip (SoC). In MCMs, multiple known-good dies are integrated, interconnected to mimic the functionality and performance of the larger SoCs. These MCMs can also combine various functionalities from different process technologies. The MEBL direct-write system is emerging as a cost-effective lithographic option for patterning substrates for these large MCM assemblies. The technology ensures the necessary resolutions to maintain performance comparable to SoCs and also enables interconnect density and bandwidth approaching metal interconnect layers in monolithic chips. This is a significant advancement in semiconductor manufacturing because it allows the industry to overcome challenges in scaling and opens new avenues for innovations. Table 1 summarizes the challenges in traditional scaling and solutions enabled by MEBL technology. It also highlights a new era of Moore’s Law, in which multicolumn e -beam pat te r n ing,  when used for “advanced integration” of chiplets, enables a large scale-up in processing capability within each package, as well as high-performance (high-bandwidth, low-power) chip-to-chip interconnects. Driving advanced integration using multicolumn e-beam lithography Adva nce d  pa ck ag i ng  a nd  MCM assembly a re  key appl icat ions for mu l t i c o lu m n  e - b e a m  p a t t e r n i n g . Compared to convent ional (single-column) elect ron-beam lithography systems, our technology enables a 100× increase in productivity because of its multiple miniaturized electron beam columns and a write-on-the-f ly motion system [6]. In addition to throughput, t he se  a dva nce me nt s  e n able  l a rge interposers of arbitrary shape and size, up to a full wafer—80x larger than today’s optical reticle. Other significant benefits include adaptable patterning (>10× higher bandwidth), interconnect density (5× improvement), on-wafer d i e - d ie  i n t eg r a t io n  (s t i t ch e d  d ie transcending reticle limit), and reduced energy per bit-transfer by >98% [7]. T hese  capabi l i t ie s ,  u n ique  to  ou r technology, enable a new generation of advanced packaging—sometimes called “advanced integration”—that delivers new levels of interconnect p e r f o r m a n c e  f o r  t o d a y ’s  f a s t e s t growing applications. Multibeam’s direct-write patterning systems a re capable of f u l l-wafer, maskless lithography with deep sub-100nm resolution and high productivity fo r  p roduc t ion  ( F i g u re  3) .  W he n p a i r e d  w i t h  D e c a  Te c h n o l o g i e s’ Adapt ive Pat ter n ing ®, Mult ibeam’s full-wafer f ield-of-view (FoV), f ine resolution, and large depth of focus dr ive advancements in chip-to-chip communication, die-shift adaptability, e n e r g y  e f f i c i e n c y ,  a n d  r a p i d development in th ree cr ucial areas that war rant fur ther elucidat ion: a) Wafer-scale interposers; b) Adaptable patterning—heterogeneous integration; and c) On-wafer d ie -d ie s t itch ing. T he se  a r e a s  a r e  d i s cu s se d  i n  t he sections below. Wa f e r - s c a l e  i n t e r p o s e r s .  B y utilizing our high-resolution and wide FoV technologies ,  it  is  possible to expand the traditional interposer size by more than 20 t imes to cover an entire wafer. This full-wafer scaling capabi l i t y  ex t end s  t he  i n t e r pose r road map sig n i f icant ly  beyond the current state-of-the-art of 3 to 4 reticle fields, enabling integration of trillions of tightly-interconnected transistors in a single device. Adaptable patterning—heterogeneous integration. Our technology can extend adaptable patterning to finer resolutions. This allows for the direct creation of distinctive die-die interconnect patterns to accommodate variations in die positions in chip-first packaging, reducing effective pad pitch and parasitic capacitances on MCM assemblies. Ultimately, this enhancement can lead to an increase in die-die interconnect bandwidth by more than 10 times while simultaneously reducing energy per bit transferred. I n  F i g u r e  4 ,  w e  e x a m i n e  o n e implementat ion case of Mult ibeam technology for advanced packaging, whereby Deca’s Adaptive Patterning® c a p a b i l i t y  c a n  b e  e x t e n d e d  f o r f u r t he r  ch ip - ch ip  com mu n ica t ion improvements.  Addit ional ly,  Deca Tech nolog ies uses Gen 2 adapt ive patterning, creating 2µm lines/spaces (L/S) with 405nm maskless laser direct imaging photolithography. Similarly, our MEBL system writes a comparable ch ip - to - ch ip  i nt e rcon nec t  pa t t e r n with 400nm lines/spaces on a silicon wafer (a 5x shrink in this example— resulting in a 5x improvement in the l inear feat u re size and beachf ront i n t e r c on ne c t  d e n s i t y) .  O u r  h ig h -re solu t ion  e -bea m ca n sca le  even further, to higher-density sub-100nm line and space redistr ibution layers, yielding a 10x or greater improvement i n  i n t e r c o n n e c t  d e n s i t y,  l e n d i n g significant performance improvements without needing to leverage complex embedded bridge chip structures. F i g u r e s  4 c - d  s h o w  s c a n n i n g electron microscope (SEM) images of 200nm lines/spaces and 30nm wide l ines, respect ively— demonst rat ing the adaptive patterning capability at f iner features. Figure 5 shows that Multibeam’s direct-write methodology a lso a l lows for  g reate r  f lex ibi l i t y i n  c h i p  d e s ig n  a n d  i n t e r c o n n e c t routing angles, including patterning of radial l ines at var ious angles—a capability that can be used to further improve interconnect impedance and capacitance. O n - w a f e r  d i e - d i e  s t i t c h i n g . I nt eg ra t ion  of  t wo or  more  d ie  i s possible on-wafer dur ing back-end-of-l ine (BEOL) processing pr ior to the sing ulat ion process.  With th is integration method, the optical reticle size is no longer a  scaling inhibitor. Fu r ther more,  d ie -d ie inte rcon nect pitch can match chip metal layer pitch, vir tually eliminating previous chip-chip communicat ion or integrat ion bottlenecks. Results of initial modeling show interconnect bandwidth between dies comparable to the performance of interconnects within a single die, which represents a significant improvement when compared to current chip-chip interconnects. This merging of dies blurs traditional advanced packaging boundaries, allowing the industry to overcome on-wafer reticle limitations, resulting in larger effective dies with unparalleled performance. Advantages of direct-write e-beam lithography Traditional photolithography, while effective for high-volume production, has become extremely capital intensive at advanced technology nodes. Also, the optical photomasks used to print p a t t e r n s  o n  w a fe r s  h ave  b e c o m e extraordinar ily complex, leading to higher development costs and longer cycle t imes. r a p id  p r o t o t y p i n g ,  p a t t e r n i n g  of i nd iv idual ly customized ch ips for design optimization, and quick cycles of learning, significantly reducing the cost associated with the development of advanced packages and speeding time to market of new products. Fu r t he r mor e ,  wh i le  t r a d i t ion a l R&D e-beam systems are extremely s low,  t he  M EBL sys t e m a ch ieve s h i g h  p r o d u c t i v i t y  w i t h  a  n e w system arch itect u re that  combines multiple miniature e-beam columns with advanced algor ithms, paral lel vector writing, write-on-the-f ly, and interferometr ic feedback cont rol to meet today’s requirements for high speed with high precision. Direct-write e-beam lithography will also play an integral role in suppor ting the next generat ions of  H BM st ack ing and integ rat ion.  The h igh precision of e-beam allows for int r icate rout ing of dense intercon nects with in and be t ween t he  s t acked s i l icon d ie s , w h i c h  w i l l  b e  n e e d e d  i n  n e x t -generation HBM devices. An inflection point for e-beam lithography The semiconductor indust r y has reached an inf lect ion point ,  where chal lenges in l ithographic scal ing, chip size limitations, and production expenses combine to hinder quick-turn chip innovation. Advanced direct-write e-beam lithography technology presents a readily deployable solution with the capability to integrate multiple high-performance central processing units (CPUs), graphics processing units Figure 6 summarizes the results of our modeling analysis of chip-chip communication electrical behavior using co-planar waveguides in LTspice®. Gen 4 in Figure 6 is enabled by Deca Technologies’ adaptive pat terning with improved overlay accuracy to eliminate pads. This represents a significant improvement over traditional chip-chip interconnects on interposers. However, much larger improvements in bandwidth density and energy efficiency are enabled with Gen 5 advanced integration. Gen 5A and 5B in Figure 6 captures MEBL-enabled advanced chip-chip communications via interposers and on-wafer die-die stitching. As the pitch and chip-chip path lengths decrease, the bandwidth density increases while energy per bit decreases. Impact of very high-density interconnects Bandwidth density—measured in g iga t ransfers per second per mm of chip-chip beachf ront—increases by nearly 10X when compared to today’s Gen 3 (f l ip-chip chip-on-interposer). Furthermore, the energy required to transmit a bit decreases by 98%, resulting in signif icant improvements in overall energy eff iciency and thermal control. These advancements in performance are crucial for coming generations of high-performance computing, edge computing, AI-at-the-edge, and other applications. Comput ing levels  requ i red to hand le these la rge and growing language models consume an astonishing amount of energy [9], 70% of which is from data bit transit alone. In addit ion to enabling low-power, high-performance communications between chiplets, high-productivity e-beam direct write can also be leveraged for additional emerging methods to improve power eff iciency and heat cont rol Figure 6: a) Results of five generations of chip-chip communications, modeled using co-planar waveguides to simulate advanced packaging; and b) A simplified RLC circuit of co-planar waveguides for electrical performance modeling of interconnects.
(GPUs), HBMs, antennas, sensors, and other devices with high-bandwidth, low-power interconnects into an optimized system (Figure 7) [11]. For chip makers, multicolumn e-beam lithography is an indispensable tool in the litho toolbox. It complements conventional opt ical l ithography to improve f lexibility, scalability, and ef f iciency in the development and production of chiplets and advanced integrated packages. An AOI solution The AOI solution discussed in this ar ticle addresses multiple inspection appl icat ions af te r  va r ious c r i t ica l steps within the AICS process f low, including the lamination of Ajinomoto bu i ld -up  f i l m (A BF),  de smea r i ng laser drilled vias, dry-film photoresist l a m i n a t i o n ,  C u  p a t t e r n i n g  a n d development, and Cu etching. We will also discuss the need for metrology solutions during this process. In our investigation, we employed an optical inspection system utilizing a high-speed focus tracking system to maintain the correct distance between the imaging optics and the area being scan ned.  Th is  is  done to keep the image focused in real time, especially on highly-warped AICSs. The system c o n t a i n s  m u l t i p l e  i l l u m i n a t i o n c h a n n e l s  t o  f i n d  h a r d - t o - d e t e c t defects, some of which are not visible under traditional brightfield (BF) and darkfield (DF) illuminations. Imaging is accomplished using a high-resolution line scan camera. The optical design provides manufacturers with tech nolog y extend ibi l i t y and the f lexibility to ut il ize the system for HVM process control, while also providing the capability to tackle R&D process opt imizat ion chal lenges in 2µm l/s. The multiple 3D metrology sensors provide addit ional data to character ize and monitor dielect r ic thickness, metal trace height, defect h e i g h t  m e a s u r e m e n t  a n d  10 0 % bump height metrology for the f inal i n t e r c o n n e c t  l a y e r .  F i n a l l y,  t h e handl ing f lex ibi l i t y of  the system al lows i t  to  accom modate copper-clad laminate (CCL) and glass-core substrates, making it well-positioned for the next technology inf lection in AICS technology. Using 2D/3D technology to overcome challenges of large-area panel inspection and metrology By Burhan Ali, Monita Pau  [Onto Innovation] he United States is seeking to breathe new life into its dome s t ic  se m iconduc t or packaging sector with the National Advanced Packaging Manufacturing Program (NAPMP), an init iat ive to “establ ish and accelerate domest ic capa c i t y  fo r  a dva nce d  pa ck ag i ng substrates and substrate mater ials,” according to a Commerce Department announcement from earlier this year [1]. In part, this program is the result of two very distinct trends, both of which are high-priority pursuits for governments and manufacturers. On the one hand, many nations like the U.S. are looking t o  s h o r e  u p  t h e i r  s e m ic o n d u c t o r manufacturing capabilit ies to bet ter protec t  t hemselves f rom potent ia l geopolitical complications. Still another consideration is today’s rapidly growing demand for high-end applications like ar tif icial intelligence (AI) and high-performance computing (HPC) that are driving the need for advanced packages with 2.5 and 3D architectures. Such st r uctures are built upon advanced integrated circuit substrates (AICS). Furthermore, the coming era of glass core substrates in advanced packaging will offer another level of challenges. The future is coming, and few want to be left behind. The role of advanced packaging M a n u f a c t u r e r s  a r e  t u r n i n g  t o a d va n c e d  p a ck a ge s  t o  he lp  t he m m e e t  t h e  i n c r e a s i n g l y  s t r i n g e n t requirements of today and tomorrow’s 2.5D and 3D packages designed for the demanding HPC and explosive A I  s e c t o r s ,  b o t h  o f  w h i c h  h a v e s p e c i f i c a t i o n s  t h a t  t r a d i t i o n a l inspection solutions struggle to meet. With each new advanced node and accompanying sh r inking t ransistor size, the single-chip approach grows more  compl ica t ed  a nd  expensive , some might even say cost prohibitive. T T hese  appl ica t ion s  have  r igorou s p e r f o r m a n c e  r e q u i r e m e n t s ,  a n d the i ndust r y  i s  t i relessly  work ing to push these technologies to thei r f u l l  potent ia l .  Each tech nolog ica l a d v a n c e m e n t  r e q u i r e s  i m p r ove d compute power with increased speed, lower latency, higher bandwidth and lower  energ y consu mpt ion .  T hese requirements lead us to chiplets. C h ip le t - ba s e d  a r ch i t e c t u r e s  on AICS offer a cost-effective alternative to the monolithic chip by serving up a f inal  sing u la r  packaged product with dies of different functionalities and nodes, thereby raising the overall functionality and device performance to the critical level for HPC and AI. All of this helps dr ive advances in panel-level packaging. The advances noted above result in a number of challenges. The amount of AICS real estate needed to serve several dies on one substrate requires large packages, as big as 150mm x 150mm cur rent ly,  with a potent ial t o  r e a ch  up  t o  24 0 m m x  24 0 m m. Additionally, each AICS may have up to 24 build-up layers, and with that comes possible complicat ions f rom panel warpage, as well as the standard k i l le r  defects that  plag ue devices. As with most things semiconductor, interconnect density is also increasing with l ine/space ( l /s)  decreasing to 5/5µm and below, pushing process control limits to the edge. In this article, we will discuss how Onto’s Firef ly® G3 inspection system, a high-sensitivity automated optical i n s p e c t i o n  (AOI )  p a n e l  s o l u t i o n c on t a i n i ng  e mb e d de d  2D a nd  3D m e t r o log y  t e ch n olog ie s ,  e n a b le s packaging houses to meet, head on, these new process control challenges posed by large, advanced packages. Included in this is the ability to better detect process excursions and f ine-t une each step of the h igh-volume
Inspection, metrology and the process flow Before we get into the par ticulars o f  t h i s  AOI  s y s t e m ,  t h e  g e n e r a l manufacturing process f low for AICS, starting from the first build-up layer, is shown in Figure 1. The defects that can be int roduced across the entire process vary based on the nature of each step. Some of the noncr it ical defects in earlier steps can evolve into killer defects at a later stage of the manufacturing process, resulting in a final device yield loss. For  t he  bu i ld -up  f i l m a nd  d r y-f i lm photoresis t  laminat ion s teps , underf ilm bubbles and par ticles can become process pain points.  Af ter t he la se r  d r i l l  and desmear  s teps , there is a need to ensure that vias are residue free. Following the patterning and development of the photoresist, r e s idue ,  ope n s  a nd  shor t s  a r e  a l l potent ial obstacles to be addressed before proceeding to the next step of Cu electrochemical plating and seed layer etching. Upon completion of the Cu plating and seed layer etching steps, Cu trace opens, shorts and seed residue add to the challenges that need to be identif ied, f irst, and then corrected. Typically, BF and DF illumination are used to identify these types of defects; however, for our purposes, we will also be exploring the use of our proprietary Clearfind® (CF) technology. Both BF and DF tech n iques use light-emitting diode (LED) sources that cover the visible wavelength spectrum. I n  BF i l lu m i n a t ion ,  t he  i m age  i s formed by the ref lected light from the sample and is a strong function of light at tenuat ion and ref lect ion between differing materials on the sample, while DF illumination is good at identifying small par ticles and defects on a f lat specular surface as it scatters the light due to the oblique angle of incidence. CF technology, however, is designed to have increased sensitivity to organic mater ial and reduced sensit ivity to inter ference f rom the su r rounding nonorgan ic  fea t u res .  T h is  c rea tes images with h igh g ray scale value (GSV) in organ ic reg ions and low GSV in nonorganic regions. Due to the nature of this illumination, CF has proven to be advantageous in several inspection steps during the build-up process by providing a strong signal- Figure 1: Typical process flow for advanced substrate build-up layers. Figure 2: Noise variation on Cu trace under different illuminations.
t o -noi se  r a t io  t o  r educe  nu i sa nce defects th rough the suppression of noise coming from the roughness of the Cu t races, while also detect ing nonvisual organic defects (Figure 2). Besides defectivity, other challenges include critical dimension variations, whe t he r  t hose  va r i a t ion s  i nvolve laser-dr illed vias, exposed pat terns on the photoresist, or uniformity of the plated features. All of these can have a signif icant impact on signal integrity and the overall performance of the f inal device. These metrology challenges will need to be addressed during research and development and before manufacturing can begin. ABF inspection after lamination and post desmear ABF is a widely used insulat ing dielectric in AICS for f lip-chip ball-grid array (FCBGA) packages. The fab environment in AICS manufacturing is not as clean as environments found in f ront-end fabs, so subst rates are exposed to foreign par ticles as they move f rom one processing tool  to another.  The foreign par t icles can potentially be embedded underneath the ABF during the lamination process, leading to the creation of defects such as shorts and opens later in the build-up process. If the embedded particle is large enough, it can lead to a layer-to-layer short that will go unnoticed until the final electrical test (E-test). Unfortunately, at this point, it is too late  to per for m any rework as the substrate has gone through multiple processing steps. While detecting embedded particles is critical, foreign particles on top of the film are considered to be nuisances because the protective film on top of the ABF is eventually removed. The challenge at this inspection step is to not only find the embedded particles, but also filter out nuisance defects from the top of the ABF so that operators don’t spend valuable time reviewing and classifying noncritical defects. A mixt u re of  DF- and CF-based imaging has proven to be beneficial in the detection of embedded defects. The combination of the two illumination channels makes the ABF translucent, thereby enabling the system to capture defec t s  a t  t he  bot tom of  t he  f i l m (Figure 3a). Because DF scatters light off particles, the system can also easily distinguish between particles on the top of the film versus the bottom, and can be classified into rough bins using on-tool binning (Figure 3b). Additional image-based classification techniques a r e  d e p l o y e d  u s i n g  p r o p r i e t a r y automated defect classification (ADC) sof t wa re ,  wh ich  u t i l i ze s  mach i ne learning to identify and classify defects into finer bins. The increasing demand to lower the amount of rework and ensure high yields on AICS has led to the implementation of inline inspection at the post-lamination step. T h e  n e e d  t o  i n s p e c t  v i a s  f o r residual ABF is also of concern. After laminat ion and cur ing of the ABF, vias are formed by high-energy CO2 or ult raviolet (UV) lasers to make electrical connections between layers. Occasionally, ABF is not completely removed f rom the  v ia  or  re sidues remain after a non-optimal desmearing p r o c e s s ,  wh ich  r e s u l t s  i n  a  p o o r electrical connection through the via after it is filled with Cu. This, in turn, negat ively af fects signal integr ity. Therefore, inspecting the via after laser ablation and desmear is necessary to find such defects. Residue is detectable using either CF or BF technology; however, CF is bet ter able to detect residual ABF inside the vias. In BF illumination, organic residue becomes less prominent because of the rough Cu present at the via bottom (Figure 4). After-development and after-etch inspection As you know, dry-film resist plays a key role in the bu i ld-up process of each layer in an AICS subst rate. Du r i ng th is  process ,  t he  d r y-f i l m resis t  i s  laminated ,  pat te r ned and developed on a Cu seed layer; any process excursions during patterning and development wil l  lead to poor circuit formation in the plating step (Figure 5). As mentioned previously, f inding all defects will require the presence of  mult iple  i l lu m i nat ion channels as organic residues are easily visible in CF, but other defects such as Figure 3: Examples of a) embedded defects, and b) on-surface particles. Figure 4: Examples of organic residue after desmear.
scratches or damage to dry film might require BF- or DF-based technology. With the development of the dry-film resist out of the way, the substrate enters the plating step where the Cu trace lines are formed. Afterward, the dry-film resist is removed, leaving all Cu trace lines connected by a thin Cu seed layer. This Cu seed layer needs to be etched away to complete circuits. It is very critical to accurately control the copper seed etching process to achieve f ine Cu t races. Under-etching may cause shorts, while over-etching may cause large reductions in the Cu trace line or result in an open circuit. All of these defects impact device reliability. When the residual Cu extends to 30% or more into the space between Cu trace lines, this is considered a critical defect. Tradit ional ly, BF-based inspect ion methodologies have been used at this inspection step, but the downside is that it results in a large number of defects, and these are often false or noncritical. This is because the surface of Cu trace lines tends to become rough and grainy af ter the etch ing process,  thereby, making any variations in the brightness of the Cu line significant. In addition, dark regions in the Cu lines can be easily mistaken for open circuits. Defect escape is also a big concern because of poor cont rast and noise f r o m  t h e  p r e s e n c e  o f  m u l t i p l e underlayers, which often shift due to process var iat ion. These challenges increase exponent ial ly as Cu l ines shrink to 2µm. Because CF technology is not sensitive to the rough or grainy surface of metal, it offers a more robust inspection technology compared to BF and DF following the after-etch step to detect defects like opens, shorts, nicks and protrusions (Figure 6). Combining inspection with the closely-integrated ADC software allows high-sensitivity i n sp e c t ion  t o  de t e c t  a nd  cl a s s i f y critical defects without the hassle of performing a manual review on a large number of defects. A not he r  key  defe c t  of  i n t e re s t , and one that  i s  a lso a  f u nct ion of an inconsistent etching process, is lef tover th in Cu seed residue. The presence of thin Cu residue becomes more prevalent as the Cu trace pitch decreases. The reason for this stems from the etching process itself. This process requires a fine-tuned etching Figure 6: Examples of opens and protrusions. Figure 5: Examples of dry-film defects.
organic film easily detectable because the metallic residue exhibits low GSV. The considerable contrast in the organic space region allows high-sensitivity sub-micron inspection to detect these small metallic particles during the after-etch inspection step. Metrology and the build-up process T he i mplement a t ion of  2D - and 3D-based metrology during the build-up process is as important as defect inspection. Such techniques are needed to ensure the process is stable during research and development and before manufacturing moves to small-volume manufacturing (SVM). Metrology is also utilized to monitor degradation as the process becomes mature in HVM. Using metrology during R&D provides additional insight into the manufacturing process at various steps. Understanding the deviations, their cause, and finding solutions with the data will enable a more suitable transition to production. Cr i t ica l  d i mensions  a re  u sed to monitor etch quality, as well as detect lens distortion in the lithography process. The AOI system employs high-resolution objectives to monitor 2µm l/s width across several points on the panel. These critical dimensions of the Cu metal line are measured for width at both the top and bottom using different illumination channels to ensure they are within specification tolerance (Figure 8). Multiple types of technology exist within the 3D metrology space that are suitable in this scenario. The AOI in this discussion contains two metrology sensors that are of use: a white light interferometry sensor and a laser triangulation sensor, with each employing a different theory of operation and each targeting different applications. The system’s white light interferometry sensor is used to monitor the thickness of the ABF as well as the height of the Cu metal line (Figures 9 and 10). The height of the metal line indicates the performance of the plating and etching process because inconsistent height can lead to signal integrity issues within the device. Meanwhile, the laser triangulation sensor enables 100% metrology for bumps/pads on the panel and is capable of determining good coplanarity before the die attach process so proper electrical connections can be formed between the chip and the AICS (Figure 11). Thin Cu seed residue is not visible i n  t r a d i t io n a l  BF-  a n d  DF- b a s e d il luminations (Figure 7b). Instead, identifying this residue requires UV-based technology. This defect is often not detectable using AOI and is typically identified using a separate review station. ABF has a high GSV when excited by CF illumination; this mimics the appearance of  U V-based imaging,  mak ing the thin Cu specular residue on top of the process and chemistry to ensure that the seed metal is completely removed between the plated Cu features, which contain a combination of larger and smaller design rules. Thin Cu residue appears as a cluster of f ine specular Cu particles in the region at the bottom of the Cu trace and on top of the ABF (Figure 7a).  If there is signif icant accumulat ion of metallic residue, a device may short. Figure 7: a) Presence of thin Cu residue; and b) Thin Cu seed residue imaged in multiple illuminations. Figure 8: Critical dimension measurements for top and bottom width of Cu line.
Summary Any nation hoping to create or revitalize domestic semiconductor production from the front end to the back end will need to devote time and resources to ensuring it has a considerable advanced packaging sector capable of meeting the needs of today and tomorrow’s 2.5 and 3D packages. To accomplish such objectives, these nations will greatly benefit from the new and emerging technologies discussed in this ar t icle as they seek ways to optimize the build-up process in advanced IC substrate manufacturing. in a package, HI involves designing multiple chips or chiplets, all of which mu s t  work  t oge t he r  i n  t he  t a rge t system, i.e., the package (Figure 2). Another difference is that traditional homogeneous chip design requires only a small amount of upf ront package planning. Detailed package design and verification is not performed until late in the application-specif ic integrated circuit (ASIC) design process, af ter the physical design is stable. Signal i nteg r it y (SI )  and power integ r it y (PI) analysis are largely decoupled from the chip design process. Because there is a single, monolithic chip, the Applying system technology co-optimization methodology for chiplet-based heterogeneous integration By Anthony Mastroianni, Todd Burkholder  [Siemens EDA] hiplets and heterogeneous i n t e g r a t i o n  ( H I )  a r e answers to semiconductor scaling challenges (Figure 1) as cost and yield are driving the development of alternatives to monolithic solutions. These solut ions include system-on-chip (SoC) disaggregation into hard intellectual property (IP), or chiplets, and multi-die implementations to avoid the diminishing returns of integrated circuit (IC) process technology scaling and reticle limitations of single dies. To take optimum advantage of this new approach, it’s essential to understand what tools and workflows are necessary to suppor t advanced packaging for heterogeneous design methods. The most basic unit  of HI is the chiplet: a die, or chip, that has been designed and optimized for integration w i t h i n  a  p a c k a g e .  T h e  n a m e “heterogeneous integration” ref lects the fact that the process integrates multiple chiplets into a single package, ca l led a  sys tem-i n-package (Si P). Chiplets may be a single die, a stacked 3D die, or a combination of multiple stacked die and single die integrated within the package. In 2.5D ICs, chiplets are mounted or embedded onto a silicon or organic substrate referred to as an interposer. T h e  i n t e r c o n n e c t  b e t w e e n  t h e assembled chiplets on the interposer, refer red to as the die-to-die (D2D) interconnect, is implemented in the inte r poser.  These ch iplet s  i nclude D2D SoC IP, such as Universal Chiplet I nte rcon nec t  Express™ ( UCIe™ ), Bunch of Wi res (BoW), and h igh-bandwidth memory (HBM). In 3D ICs, chiplets are stacked vertically on top of each other. The interconnect in 3D ICs is made vertically from one chiplet to the other. General ly,  2 .5D ICs and 3D ICs are, in turn, mounted on an organic package substrate, which is used to make the connections to the external C pins of the package. Traditional high-speed interfaces (such as double data rate [DDR],  Per ipheral Component I n t e rcon nec t  Express  ( PCIe),  a nd universal serial bus [USB]) from the package to it s  host  pr inted ci rcu it board (PCB), commonly refer red to as chip-to-chip (CTC) interfaces, are implemented with in the package’s ch iplets using SoC IP, or they can be implemented as stand-alone CTC interface chiplets integrated as par t of the package. These CTC interfaces are connected through external pins of the package and used to connect the package to other ICs or components on the PCB. The combination of the high-speed i n t e r c o n n e c t i o n s  a n d  p a c k a g i n g tech nolog ies noted above del ivers orders of magnitude improvements i n  p e r fo r m a nce ,  a r e a ,  a nd  p owe r usage because everything is so close together, compared to having to go off chip, and, naturally, it takes much less time and energy to travel these vastly smaller spaces. A  k e y  d i f f e r e n c e  b e t w e e n  t h e homogeneous (t r ad it ional)  and HI IC design f lows is that ,  instead of designing a single chip and putting that Figure 1: Today’s heterogeneously-integrated semiconductor packages enable dramatic increases in bandwidth and performance with reduced power and cost. Figure 2: Example of a 3D IC.
power and thermal profiles within the package are relatively simple, making thermal analysis and management straight forward, which can be readily outsourced to a contractor or outsourced semiconductor and test (OSAT) supplier. Overall, the collaboration between the package design team and the system, register-transfer level (RTL), chip, and design-for-test (DFT)/test teams is minimal. O n  t h e  c o n t r a r y,  H I  d e s i g n  r e q u i r e s  s i g n i f i c a n t collaborat ion between the package design team and the system, RTL, chiplet , and DFT/test teams. Notably, the system and RTL architects must navigate through a broad set of design implementations. Internal die-to-die (D2D) interfaces and the associated internal D2D SI and PI need to be considered in defining the optimal micro-architecture. The packaging technology and chiplet technology nodes, as well as thermal considerations, must be factored into the evaluation. Perhaps the greatest challenge lies in testing the multiple dies within a package. HI yields tremendous benefits, however, it is a much more complicated design process. To be accomplished efficiently, the tools and workf lows used must work well together. This requires a co-design environment using multiple tools— including packaging, thermal stress, chiplet, and DFT—as well as collaboration between the package team and all other design and verification teams. Fortunately, most of the tools already exist to implement these workf lows,  but putting them together in a cohesive workf low presents a significant hurdle because the tools for packaging, thermal analysis, SI, design for manufacturing (DFM), etc., are distinct and highly specialized. The answer is a system technology co-optimization (STCO) methodology that decomposes the system design into multiple chiplets. STCO enables architectural and technology trade-offs that achieve high-performance, cost-effective solutions in the shortest period of time. A fundamental component of the STCO methodology is predictive modeling that leverages the modeling tools during physical planning to gain early insight into downst ream performance. This knowledge enables corrective action, if needed, at a point in the process when it’s easiest to make changes. STCO-driven HI workflows In the remainder of this article, we will describe five workflows essential to a successful STCO-driven, HI methodology (Figure 3). These workf lows, discussed in the sections below, are: 1) Architectural planning and analysis; 2) Physical design planning and analysis; 3) Electrical design analysis; 4) Reliability design analysis; and 5) Test planning and validation. Architectural planning and analysis. The design process starts with architectural planning (Figure 4), which involves deciding how to decompose a system into multiple chiplets. The architect analyzes the system and decides how to break out the different functionalities and implement those in the chiplets. Many trade-offs need to be made, with the goal of determining a viable set of microarchitectures and then identifying the optimal one. This planning and predictive analysis should be done upfront—i.e., during the architectural phase—where these decisions have the most impact on the design and enable design architects to implement the best architecture in terms of packaging and chiplet technologies.
One of the big advantages of HI is that this approach enables companies to pay for a particular piece of silicon only where they need it. For example, for an analog chiplet, they can integrate a process technology optimized for analog performance, which may be considerably less expensive than an advanced digital technology node. On the other hand, if very high performance is required, the architect will choose more expensive, bleeding-edge technology, and these can be mixed and matched. The d if ferent microarchitectu ral scenar ios a re then provided to the packaging team so that they can start doing the initial f loorplanning (i.e., where to place the different chiplets) and start looking at which packaging technology is most appropriate to meet the performance requirements of those interfaces. For example, is a silicon interposer needed to implement the routing channels to achieve the speeds required for the interfaces, or will an organic interposer suffice? Additionally, once they floorplan the chiplet, they can look at thermal performance and decide what kind of cooling system is needed and start planning the internal power delivery network. Typically, the architect has a good understanding of the performance and functionality of the system, but they don’t necessarily have a lot of knowledge about the various packaging technologies. And because these are the kind of trade-offs in which a traditional RTL architect would not have expertise, collaboration is Figure 4: Flow 1—architectural planning and analysis. essential even at this point in the overall design flow. By using predictive analysis models early, the teams can figure out what produces the most desirable power, performance, area, cost, and form factor. P h y s i c a l  d e s i g n  p l a n n i n g a n d  a n a l y s i s .  O n c e  t h e  o p t i m a l microarchitecture has been selected, t h e  a c t u a l  f l o o r p l a n n i n g  o f  t h e chiplets proceeds in preparat ion of implementation (Figure 5). Only when the architecture has been selected should implementation of the chiplets begin because it is expensive to go back and make changes. A n  i m p o r t a n t  c o n s id e r a t io n  i s how to deliver the power for each of the chiplets. Because the power will eventually come out through the bottom subst rate f rom the board, subst rate planning is performed to determine how to get all the IOs out and connected to  each of  t he  ch iple t s ,  i nclud i ng power delivery. This connectivity is very important and complex because HI system and package designs have multiple chips that must be connected— with hundreds of thousands, and soon, millions of connections. The f loorplan designers need to be able to define and simulate all of these connections to end up with a complete and accurate functional netlist. Layout-vs-schematic (LVS) checks mus t  be  u sed  to  va l id a t e  t ha t  t he connections to be implemented in the physical design are identical to the netlist connectivity defined by the RTL design team for the package-level interconnects. The high-speed interfaces need to be carefully aligned and balanced for good signal integrity. Any one chip designer does not have visibility into the other chiplets that are being connected to their chiplet. Using package planning tools with visibility of the chiplet IO, the package team leader can perform the SiP-level floorplanning and coordinate the IO placement within each of the chiplets to achieve good alignment of the functional IO, the high-speed interface, and the test IO. Again, a highly collaborative process needs to be supported. Early predictive analysis is applied again toward selecting the right architecture and the right packaging technology upfront. Once the architecture, chiplet IC technologies, and package technologies are defined, the detailed physical design planning and layout of the chiplets, interposer, and substrate are performed concur rently, running ver if icat ions throughout the process. Electrical design analysis. Detailed e l e c t r i c a l  d e s ig n  a n a ly s i s  of  t h e chiplets and the complete SiP design are required during the physical design i m ple m e n t a t io n  ( F i g u r e  6 ) .  T h e focus of this workf low is to perform predict ive, in-design, and signoff-level electrical analysis of HI designs that are comprised of silicon, organic, and possibly type III/V semiconductor components. Both organic and silicon parasitic extraction (PEX) tools and workflows are required to support these electrical analysis workflows. The HI electrical analysis workflows include SiP-level SI and PI analysis, static and dynamic IR drop analysis, elect romigrat ion, and stat ic t iming PRoHS
greater design challenges. In both cases, thermal coupling needs to be carefully planned and simulated to make sure there are neither thermal nor reliability issues. Thermal-mechanical stress is also very important in these designs. There are different coefficient of thermal expansion values between sil icon and organic structures. This can induce warpage, which could lead to manufactur ing Reliability analysis. Reliability analysis includes thermal and thermal-mechanical st ress and war page analyses using integrated multi-physics simulation tools (Figure 7). In 2.5D applications, thermal coupling between adjacent chiplets can cause problems with the operation of the device. Additionally, thermal coupling of stacked-die 3D ICs is inherently much greater than in 2.5D designs, creating even analysis (STA). Back annotated timing simulations can also be supported by leveraging the STA workf low. Early, pre-layout analysis is supported using pred ic t ive ext ract ion and analysis workf lows. In-design analysis enables r a p id  a n a ly s i s  d u r i ng  t he  d e s ig n implementation phase. Full signoff checks and analysis support releasing the design to manufacturing handoff. Figure 5: Flow 2—physical design planning and analysis. Figure 6: Flow 3—electrical design analysis.
interconnect defects or potential, longer-term reliability issues. These effects become more pronounced in larger silicon interposer-based designs, requir ing detailed thermal stress analysis. To mitigate these issues, package assembly design rules are developed to reduce the impact of the stress on the die-to-substrate, die-to-interposer, and die-to-die connections. Additional mechanical structures, such as package lids or stiffeners, are required to mitigate the warpage issues. This also needs to be carefully planned and simulated to make sure there are no warpage or mechanical stress reliability issues. Reliability analysis requires detailed physical dimension and material properties as well as boundary conditions to support simulations. Power models for a chiplet can be used to simulate the thermal heat sources of the active components, or pre-characterized chiplet thermal models can be provided and used in SiP-level analysis. A boundary condition independent reduced order model (BCIE-ROM) can be created for the entire SiP model for use in board- and system-level thermal simulations. In addit ion to thermal and st ress analyses, DFM design methods and verification tools should be run on the chiplet, substrate, and interposers to ensure best practices are used to optimize the manufacturing yield and long-term reliability of a SiP device. Electrical rule checking tools are also required to check for electrical reliability issues such as latch-up, electrostatic discharge damage, or both, at the chiplet and SiP levels. Te st  p lan n i ng  and va l idat ion . Finally, we move to the test planning and validation part of the overall design flow (Figure 8). There are well-established DFT standards, methods, and tools to perform structural testing of individual chips and chiplets. These DFT structures must  be designed into each of the chiplets. Because most of the chiplet pins are not exposed to the external package IO, new DFT test standards, methods, and tools have been developed to support the manufacturing test of 2.5D and 3D IC designs. These tools can also support structural, functional, and diagnostic testing of SiP devices during operation in a system. All of the DFT test methods need to be planned up front and implemented at the chiplet and package levels. For existing reusable chiplet components, it is imperative that the required DFT structures are included in the device, otherwise it may not be possible to test the individual chiplets within a packaged design, which is a requirement in many applications and markets. Test IO needs to be planned early in the architectural and design planning phases. The required test st rategies and associated die and SiP-level test IO planning and interconnects can be suppor ted through collaborat ion between the package team lead and the DFT and test teams. The STA/ timing simulation workf lows can be leveraged to verify the timing of these test interfaces. Prior to the package assembly process, the individual chiplet components are pre-tested at the wafer level to identify manufacturing defects. This is referred to  a s  wafe r-level  t e s t i ng or  wafe r sorting. Unlike traditional homogeneous SoC device test pins, the pins on HI devices cannot be directly accessed with a test probe card because they are too small. Therefore, to facilitate wafer sort testing of HI devices, larger test pads must be added to the chiplet die and internally connected to the associated test and power and ground pins. The integrated IR drop f lows can also be leveraged to ver ify that the power delivery network can supply adequate power to the chiplets in both functional and test modes. Putting it all together HI technology enables design teams to build larger systems than can be done with monolithic designs and can deliver improved power, performance, area, and form factors. These benefits Figure 7: Flow 4—reliability analysis.
do not come without a cost, requiring a comprehensive set of integrated, multi-domain tools and workflows to support the concurrent design of a broad set of design disciplines, including architectural planning and analysis, IC design, package design, electrical analysis, multi-physics simulation, and DFT/test. Most of the required tools exist today, but they have not been designed to work together in a seamless manner as required in HI designs. New tools such as true-3D place-and-route are currently under development to support the design and verification of stacked-die designs. The increased size and complexity of these designs also require many of the tools to be adapted to address these size and complexity challenges as well as support new standards and interfaces to enable the concurrency and interoperability requirements of these integrated workflows. These integrated work f lows a lso need to  suppor t  a diverse set of design kits required in each of the design domains, leveraging new and emerging machine-readable formats. These design kits will require a collaborative approach to create a set of common assembly rules and material properties that can support the multi-technology, multi-vendor data used in specific 3D IC designs. Ideally, these formats, should be machine readable and electronic design automation (EDA) vendor neutral, enabling different tools, scripts, and workf lows to leverage a common set of design kits. The workf lows noted above wil l require advanced data management systems to manage the storage and interdependencies of all this mult i- domain data, including design IP, design kits, intermediate design data, verification and analysis results, and the final handoff to manufacturing deliverables, including final design databases, test patterns, and design collateral. Summary HI design workf lows must facilitate a collaborative design environment to enable the diverse set of design teams to work concurrently throughout the entire design process. This approach is much different than the traditional homogeneous ASIC design process where many of the different design processes can be run independently in a more serial design process. Working together, enterprise design, verification, and test teams can reap the benefits of the cutting-edge HI technology. Figure 8: Flow 5—test planning and validation. 10-15µm. This process is executed within a single-chamber tool, showcasing very high time-resolved thermal control levels and spatial precision [4,5]. Here, we describe some of the reflow defect challenges we encountered and successfully resolved. As the density of areas increases and the diameter of pillars decreases, the challenges of removing SnO/SnO2 and CuO/CuO2 and of their salt formats by-products are becoming even more apparent. With a reduction in solder ball diameter from 50-7µm, there is an approximate 50x decrease in t in oxide volume per pillar, making it more challenging to identify the optimal process point and causing the process window (illustrated in Figures 1, 4, 5) to narrow progressively. The increase in areal density (roughly 28 times) can lead to a volumetric increase in copper oxide on the pillars and as a by-product in the gas phase. Impact of fluxless vacuum reflow on bump defects for fine-pitch packaging applications By Xinxuan Tan, Zia Karim, Vladimir Kudriavtsev, Tapani Laaksonen, Lei Jing, Chris Lane  [Yield Engineering Systems, Inc.] cal ing dow n microbu mps involves a holistic strategy— one that requires integrating exper t i se  f rom mate r ia l s  sc ience , process engineering, and cutting-edge technologies to overcome the hurdles of achieving sub-10µm  pitch in solderless ref low procedures. This approach not only capitalizes on existing Cu pillar microbump structures, but also encourages equipment suppliers to innovate and offer tailored technical solutions. This study  delves into the intricacies of fine-pitch microbumps and various defects— descr ibing thei r  requ i rements and developmental obstacles, and investigating process results via system parameters, surface morphology analysis, and digital processing methodologies. Microbumps Bump density, as well as I/O density, increases with the scaling of bump pitch [1]. Hybrid bonding will be the technology that will be adopted for bump pitch applications at 10µm or below. In his review article, Mark Lapedus [2] provided an intricate examination of advancements in microbump pitches. In contemporary advanced packag i ng,  m ic robu mps typically feature a 40µm pitch, translating to bump sizes of 20-25µm with 15µm spacing between adjacent bumps on the die. Our work was conducted with pillars having diameters of 10-12µm and pillar-to-pillar distances ranging from 10-15µm. Forecasts indicate that existing bump technologies with solder-based interconnects will progress to accommodate a 10µm pitch (5µm pillar) [2]. Various alternative methods are vying for prominence, including hybrid bonding, thermocompression bonding (TCB) with flux, TCB with fluxless clean, and fluxless dry thermal reflow (Figure 1). As high-bandwidth memory (HBM) has evolved over the last two years, solder microbumps from HBM1 (4 memory chips, 8Gb), HBM2 (2018, 4-8 memory chips, 16Gb), and HBM3 (2022, 8-12 memory S chips, 24-36Gb) have been utilized. Even for HBM4 (2025-26, 12-16 memory chips, 36-64Gb), in most cases, it will be solder based, with probably less than 30% being done using hybrid bonding [3]. Accord i ng  to  [2] ,  t he  emerg i ng developments highlight pillar bumps with an 18µm pitch, featuring 9µm diameters and 20µm heights, amounting to 200 million bumps per 300mm wafer. Some sources suggest a 10µm pitch with 5µm diameters and 10µm heights, leading to 500 million bumps. The trend towards finer pitches has reduced copper height and solder cap size. Hypothetically, this could manifest as a 3µm copper layer, a 3µm nickel barrier, and a 5µm solder cap [2]. Notably, dry fluxless formic acid reflow, absent in Lapedus’ analysis [2], has seen successful implementation by the present authors at solder ball diameters of 10µm and below, with pillar-to-pillar distances of Figure 1: Solder bump fluxless reflow process window [time*energy].
Recent papers presented at ECTC 2023, 2024 [4] and IMAPS 2023 [5] discussed the implementation of wafer processing with ~10µm pillar diameter. They developed methods for temperature uniformity control, process development, testing, and metrology using a tool platform developed by YES. These papers explored [TimeEnergy] product tradeoffs, and in particular, focused on sub-atmospheric formic acid pre-clean and low-pressure (vacuum) thermal reflow processes.As we aim to meet mass volume production requirements (MWP) for higher throughput, the optimal process window for  t i n  ox ide reduct ion is becoming narrower. Balancing the need to decelerate the tin oxide reduction process rate to maintain precision with MWP is a challenge, prompting a continuous effort to shorten the tin oxide reduction step. This involves increasing the formic acid partial pressure (operating chamber pressure), which extends the time to reach operational pressure, necessitates a smaller chamber volume, and raises the risk of reactants and by-product condensation on chamber walls, potentially causing defects.To prevent product condensation and improve reaction rates, raising the formic acid vapor and wafer temperatures during the soak step seems logical [7,8]. However, this can create thermal gradient issues affecting process uniformity. Rapid tin oxide removal rates can lead to uneven removal across the wafer, with potential over-exposure leading to copper oxide removal from the pillars. This can cause wicking and issues during the ref low step if solder balls drop or flow down the pillars due to the absence of copper oxide (Figure 4). In summary, both process steps are interrelated and influenced by the [TimeEnergy] product, each with its unique process paths and challenges. Equipment and process Our strategy is to eliminate tin oxide before the reflow stage while preserving copper oxide. We use a process gas that exhibits a higher reduction rate for tin oxide compared to copper oxide. Formic acid (HCOOH) gas has demonstrated excellent selectivity in this regard, with tin oxide being reduced approximately five times faster than copper oxide [6-8]. The specific reduction rate ratio depends on the oxidation states of these metals, as both copper (Cu) and tin (Sn) can exist in two oxidation states [7]. Also, formic acid can exist as a dimer and a monomer. The addition of a nickel layer (nickel oxide) [2] can further complicate matters. Cu2O is the native oxide that forms on the copper surface after a brief exposure to air, while CuO develops after a longer exposure time [5,6]. Our process employs the high-volume production chamber developed by YES (VeroTherm™ FAR), as detailed in [4-6]. This dual single-chamber tool operates under varying degrees of vacuum, ranging from sub-atmospheric levels down to 1Torr, ensuring minimal exposure of the product to the ambient environment. Figure 2 shows an example of reflow performance for an 11µm diameter solder cup and its shape variations. Results were obtained using shape digitization techniques. We heat the wafer using a high ramp rate heat source while maintaining uniform temperature distribution through LEADERS IN  MICRO DISPENSING TECHNOLOGY Our Micro Dispensing product line is proven and trusted by manufacturers in semiconductor, electronics assembly, medical device and electro-mechanical assembly the world over. www.dltechnology.com. 216 River Street, Haverhill, MA 01832  •  P: 978.374.6451  •  F: 978.372.4889  •  info@dltechnology.com SMALL REPEATABLE VOLUMES ARE A CHALLENGE, BUT NOT IMPOSSIBLE IF YOU HAVE BEEN CREATING THEM AS LONG AS WE HAVE. our proprietary mechanism [4]. Chemical delivery is facilitated through a dissipator tube inside the chamber, creating a highly effective mixing vortex due to the cylindrical chamber geometry and our proprietary f luid delivery system. Adjacent parts are heated to prevent by-product condensation, and formic acid is pre-heated to the desired temperature. A responsive thermal control system monitors the wafer’s backside temperature, adjusting the power on a multi-zone heating module to achieve an optimal time-dependent thermal trajectory. Results presented in Figure 3 demonstrate soak and reflow uniformity within ±1.5°C, and better incremental improvements can be achieved with further development. Reflow occurs under low pressure (vacuum) after evacuating all formic acid by-products from the chamber. The process is quenched using a cold gas provided by a substrate cooling mechanism. Defect characterization We  o b s e r v e d  v a r i o u s  d e f e c t mechanisms, such as under- or over-ref low, wicking, skewing, voids, poor wet t ing, f lak ing, and intermetal l ic compound (IMC) formations during the f luxless formic acid process [1]. These observations were made based on microbumps with a diameter of 10μm and below. We correlated the ref low process window to defect mapping and temperature uniformity using an optical microscope, scanning electron microscope (SEM), and automatic optical inspection techniques. This resulted in a clear process window with 100% defect-free bumps and good reliability. In Table 1, we present an image-based score classif ication system. It can be easily automated using image processing algorithms with edge functions and area ratio calculations between white and black areas. T h e  r e f l ow  p r o c e s s  b e g i n s  b y evacuating the system to a low vacuum (0.1Torr~300Torr) to create an oxygen-free processing environment. A controlled concentration of formic acid chemical vapor is applied to the wafer for solder surface oxide (SnO and SnO2) removal. Additionally, nitrogen dilution gas is introduced into the chamber through the dissipator. The chamber gas temperature is maintained within the range of 150°C to 180°C (Figure 1). Chemical vapor Table 1: Solder reflow performance evaluation score card. Figure 2: Example of digitized bump shape analysis (bump diameter 11µm). Six neighboring bumps are processed and superimposed in the same coordinate system. Average Rt=H/D=1.00577, H/D uniformity is 5.9% Figure 3: Temperature uniformity on wafer for: a) soak (±1°C); and reflow (+1.5°C).
is injected into the chamber through nozzles, controlled either by a bubbler or vaporizer. The chemical mixture is dispersed over the substrate through convec t ive  m i x i ng a nd d i f f u sion , wh ich is  simulated and opt imized using 3D reacting f low computational f luid mechanics (ANSYS FLUENT/ CFX). After exposing the wafer to the chemical vapor of formic acid, the excess vapor and by-product metal formats are removed from the chamber with the vacuum. The wafer support positions the wafer for heating, targeting a temperature of 230°C~250°C for the reflow process. Once ref low/soldering is complete, the heating sources are either turned off or reduced to safe idle power and the wafer is rapidly cooled down with convective gas flow. Metrology method and key findings While measurement companies strive to tackle the challenges associated with full-wafer coplanarity measurements, we have developed a practical and robust method for conducting our experiments and characterizing reflow performance. Our methodology centers on the use of the x2500 microscopy tool (Keyence VHX-7000). This tool, however, has a limitation— it cannot accommodate a full wafer under the microscope and is primarily suitable for examining coupons or smaller pieces cut from a complete wafer. To assess bump performance comprehensively, SEM was employed to document surface characteristics and cross-sectional features. A statistical analysis, utilizing digital image processing techniques, was conducted, leading to the development of color-coded cards. Each color card was assigned distinct values to provide a qualitative assessment of bump performance. This approach offers a visually intuitive representation of the analyzed data, facilitating a deeper understanding of the variations and trends in bump characteristics. The specific combinations outlined in Table 1, along with surface morphology images, underwent a comprehensive statistical analysis through digital processing, resulting in the generation of a performance scorecard. Any condit ions yielding scores below 90% were rejected. This analysis mapped variations in bump shape concerning soaking temperature, soaking time, reflow temperature, and reflow time. Additionally, it delved into the mechanisms governing solder melting and flowing.
