Figure 4 visually depicts the progression of formic acid reflow patterns from soaking initiation to under-reflow and complete reflow, observing various surface features. Different scores and acceptance criteria aligned with the subjective evaluation were based on the reflow progress. Table 1 delineates the process tuning range, balancing exposure time and substrate temperature. For instance, Figure 4 illustrates the 90% completion of reflow indicated by the green line, where the corresponding green region signifies 100% reflow completion. Conversely, when the soak temperature is set at the normalized temperature A with an X exposure time, reflow completion is less than 80%, manifesting distinct defect indications such as a rough bump surface and potential void formation. Adjusting the process conditions to (A+15) °C (see Figure 5) and 1.5X time rectified these defects, achieving 100% complete reflow. Figure 5 illustrates the process window of reflow soldering, typically occurring within the temperature range of approximately 230°C to 250°C, and the pressure less than 300Torr, which is quite different from standard ambient pressure reflow. The process windows (time, pressure and temperature) within this range are narrower than the soaking step, necessitating consideration for under-reflow and over-reflow scenarios. Under-ref low instances primarily manifest on the lower temperature side, resulting in minimal alteration in solder bump morphology because of inadequate heat transfer to the metal. Conversely, overf low scenarios occur at significantly higher temperatures, leading to the onset of crystalline structure formation on the metal surface and increased surface roughness. For example, as depicted in Figures 4 and 5, reflow conducted at the normalized temperature A, with 30% of B times, is classified as under-reflow. However, increasing the temperature to A+15°C, or extending the reflow time to 50% of B time, achieves 100% reflow completion. Figure 5: Process window: pressure and temperature. Figure 6: Defects with respect to: a) surface smoothness (pitches); and b) IMC layer. Figure 4: Substrate time/temperature trade-offs and pillar reflow results.
Our optical imaging of microbumps and digital analysis proved highly effective in delivering precise ref low shape measurements down to an accuracy of 0.25µm [5], as depicted in Figure 2. Surface pitch represents another stress-induced defect observed during SnOx soaking and solder reflow. Figure 6a illustrates this specific bump defect alongside a well-formed bump exhibiting a smooth surface. Due to the limitations of our optical microscope, a more detailed evaluation of these defects necessitates using a SEM. Figure 6b showcases the effect of thicker and uneven IMC, attributed to issues such as an uneven distribution of Ag (internal dendrite) or nonuniform spread of Ag in SAC (Sn/Ag/Cu) solder balls, along with material defects induced by reoxidation post-ref low or the presence of IMC formations. To mitigate these issues, we implemented formic acid ref low under vacuum and utilized specialized heat sources combined with rapid cooling capability, proving effective in reducing solder-induced stresses and surface defects. In our specific manufacturing process, we optimize thermal energy by fine-tuning ref low temperature and duration to alleviate this defect mode. This success is attributed to meticulous control of soak temperature, ramp, and cooling rate inside a single-chamber environment. Following the statistical analysis, a process condition demonstrating 100% bump performance was selected and implemented across the wafer. An extensive automated optical inspection (AOI) map showcasing a >99% yield is provided in Figure 7 (coplanarity [COP] and bump height variations), highlighting the success of our defect-free f luxless solder ref low process. This outcome underscores the effectiveness and reliability of the chosen process condition in achieving optimal bump performance across the entire wafer substrate. Summary We have demonstrated the feasibility of mass-producing 10-12µm diameter SAC microbumps with <1% thermal uniformity and without defects. This was achieved through a vacuum-based ref low process executed in a thermally-controlled single-chamber tool, encompassing soak, ref low, and cooldown in a single sequence. Optical microscopy, SEM, and AOI were pivotal in studying and digitizing reflow shapes, correlating them with the formic acid soak and ref low’s thermal energy product. Dr. Tan has worked in the semiconductor equipment, solar, and PCB industry and academia for over 10 years, with a wealth of experience in developing, inventing, and advancing PVD systems, backend packaging equipment and the novel device & thin film material characterization methodologies. 60°C at the aforement ioned failure modes in the legacy activation energy 0.7eV can increase or decrease the mean time to failure by a factor of 30 [4]. Based on the findings noted above, it begs the question of applicability of the legacy activation energy of 0.7eV for lifetime prediction of organic (polymeric) material sets, which involves a complex and dynamic interaction of chemistry. Generally, activation energy of polymer-based materials is reported in the range of 10-80kcal/mol or 0.434eV to 3.47eV (depending on the environment) [5]. This wide range (i.e., as compared to the legacy 0.7eV value used as default) reinforces the importance of honing in use of the appropriate activation energy value associated with the specific failure mode in lifetime prediction calculations. As an example, the solder mask is a material comprised within a FCBGA Activation energy for an organic packaging material set for flip-chip applications By Jaimal Williamson  [Texas Instruments] rganic substrates continue t o  p r o l i f e r a t e  a s  t h e chip car r ier for var ious semiconductor devices because of their high routing density and lower relative cost. Because of this proliferation, there is a burgeoning need to predict lifetimes of these material sets. For example, the versatility of flip-chip devices (Figure 1) assembled to organic substrates support a myriad of applications ranging from automotive, industrial, aerospace and defense to wireless infrastructure. As a reference, the f lip-chip market size has a compound annual growth rate (CAGR) of 6.8% projected from 2022 to 2027 [1]. Understanding the mission profiles at continuous use temperatures at specific powers-on hours (POH) is of keen interest for both integrated device manufacturers (IDMs) and or iginal equipment manufacturers (OEMs) of flip-chip devices. A key path to establishing the eff icacy of the mission profile is deriving an effective activation energy to calculate lifetime prediction. For instance, with the advent of elect r ic vehicles (EV) where semiconductors are active during charging, the need to predict the thermo-mechanical reliability of the organic packaging material set (derived from an empirically-derived activation energy) is essential to establishing an automotive mindset with a focus on zero defects. Deriving an activation energy for packaging material sets becomes critical as it enables an elucidation of acceleration factors associated with applications greater than or equal to 100K POH at continuous use temperatures greater than or equal to 110°C. Wit h  r e s p e c t  t o  t h e  l i t e r a t u r e , finding an activation energy to predict fa i lu re  modes  a s soc ia t ed  w i t h  a n organic packaging material set (i.e., glass-f iber reinforced core, si l ica-f il led ABF build-up dielect r ic, and solder mask as depicted in Figure 2 of a f lip-chip ball-grid array (FCBGA) O substrate) is obscure and ostensibly not  r ea d i ly  ava i lable  fo r  l i fe t i me prediction purposes. As an example, J E P12 2 H  J E DE C  p u b l i c a t io n  fo r Failure Mechanism and Models for Semiconductor Devices  [2],  l is t s a mult itude of act ivat ion energies of different failure modes. However, there is no notation for thermal oxidative degradation mechanisms causing bulk underfill fillet cracking, which can cause a failure mode of crack propagation into the organic substrate. For many years, the legacy activation energy of 0.7eV for silicon reliability has been used as a general  r u le for  va r ious failure modes. These failure modes can include intermetallic growth, corrosion generated from metal migration, and electromigration, to name a few [3]. However, it has been documented that an erroneous calculation of 0.1eV at Figure 1: Example of a FCBGA package. Figure 2: Example of a FCBGA substrate.
substrate as depicted in Figure 2. Similar to the build-up dielect r ic and core material, the solder mask is a composite of materials formulated with monomers, polymers, silica filler, etc. Because of the composite structure, the calculation of the activation energy for solder mask decomposition can be a challenging task. This challenge manifests because a first-order Arrhenius approximation may not be applicable to the system due to the composite make-up of the materials involved in the chemical reaction. This article focuses on a study deriving the act ivat ion energy of an organic material set as correlated to its specific failure mechanism and consequently, its failure mode. The test vehicle was a FCBGA package subjected to long-term isothermal exposure at three different high-temperature storage level (HTSL) conditions. Although the aforementioned text referred to activation energy in terms of a chemical reaction process (i.e., degradation), the context of the activation energy extracted in this study refers to the minimum amount of energy required to induce a temperature-accelerated failure mechanism. The empirically-derived activation energy specific to a thermal oxidative degradation failure mechanism is compared to the legacy 0.7eV activation energy reported to be unreliable leading to erroneous reliability predictions. As an output, the number of stress hours needed to simulate a 100K POH application at a continuous use temperature of 110°C was compared using the empirically-derived activation energy and legacy value based on the Arrhenius equation. Approach in deriving the activation energy A lidded FCBGA package at a body size of 17mm x 17mm with six build-up dielectric layers and a 400-micron core thickness was subjected to a series of HTSL component-level testing to der ive the act ivat ion energy. Three Table 1: Description of the organic FCBGA substrate evaluated focusing on chemical structure-to-material property relationships. Figure 3: Example of a failure mechanism as a result of thermal oxidative degradation.
different HTSL conditions of 150°C, 160°C, and 175°C were investigated to trigger the temperature-accelerated failure mechanism. Two high-volume manufacturing (HVM) core and solder mask materials comprising the organic subst rate were evaluated.  Organ ic substrate material selection focused on the chemical structure-to-material property relationship to address the high POH and continuous use temperature requi rements. Table 1  summar izes the description of the organic FCBGA subst rate used for the study. Equal sample sizes of the FCBGA parts were each submit ted to HTSL test ing of 150°C, 160°C, and 175°C. Parts were electrically tested to failure at periodic read points of 500 hours. Upon the first failure, a detailed failure analysis was conducted including cross-sectional analysis and planar polishing of substrate layers where applicable to isolate the failing interface or material. Results and discussion In general, HTSL testing creates an environment that facilitates additional crosslinking of polymeric mater ials as the high temperature can diffuse polymeric chains into entanglements. Ther mal analysis such as dynamic m e c h a n i c a l  a n a l y s i s  ( DM A)  h a s demonstrated that the glass transition temperature (Tg) of a polymeric material like an epoxy mold compound (EMC) or underfill steadily increases upon aging [6,7]. Further aging causes the Tg to plateau and cause material embrittlement. This initiates bulk material cracks (e.g., underfill) that originate from thermal oxidative degradation during isothermal aging. The bulk underf i l l cracking is driven by the increased brittleness of the polymer as the polymer chains become more  ent a ng led .  Poly mer chain entanglement limits molecular mobility and oxygen diffusion during the crosslinking reaction. It is a common visual during HTSL testing to observe color differences (i.e., a darkening) of FCBGA parts at the underfill fillet region illustrating the effect of oxidation. The image in Figure 3 shows the degree of oxidation penetrating the underfill fillet as evidenced by the brown line. Correspondingly, Figure 4 illustrates another vantage point of a top-down view of the underfill fillet cracks stemming from long-term isothermal aging. Thermal oxidative degradation is the primary failure mechanism for electrical failures found from this study as the underfill and solder mask tend to be the vulnerable materials within the substrate. The underfill and solder mask are most exposed within the package during reliability testing. Although the underfill is protected mostly by the lid and silicon die (as illustrated in Figure 1), the underfill fillet is prone to attack by oxygen. Once the underfill is compromised because of the thermal oxidative degradation mechanism and exhibits bulk cracking, the underlying solder mask falls in the crack propagation path, meaning that it runs into the adjacent material. Figure 5 shows an example of the failure mode of substrate cracking caused from thermal oxidative degradation driving bulk material cracking in the underfill and solder mask. The following sections discuss various aspects of the results obtained from the study. Hypothesis on leg 1 and leg 2 dif ferences.  A JMP stat is t ical  sof tware rev iew of the accelerat ion factor stemming from package-level HTSL testing of leg 1 and leg 2 splits revealed expected differences comparing the extreme HTSL conditions of 150°C and 175°C. JMP-derived acceleration factors showed leg 1 (Figure 6) with the first fail at 175°C occurring ~2x earlier than the first failure at 150°C. In contrast, the JMP-derived acceleration factors showed leg 2 (Figure 7) with the first fail at 175°C occurring ~4x earlier than the first failure at 150°C. Based on the acceleration factor of leg 2 with one hour in the accelerated condition of 175°C being equivalent roughly to four hours at the reference 150°C condition (i.e., whereas two hours being equivalent roughly in the case of leg 1 at reference temperature), the improved material properties of the organic packaging material set can be tied directly to this acceleration factor. The hypothesis for the improved performance of leg 2 is because of the Figure 6: Acceleration factor of leg 1 for 175°C HTSL condition (based on a use condition of 150°C). Figure 4: Top down view of underfill filet cracks post extended HTSL testing as a result of thermal oxidative degradation.

Eq. 1 For compar ison, both the legacy (i.e., baseline) and activation energies derived from extended HSTL testing were used to simulate stress conditions equivalent to a 100K POH environment at continuous use temperatures of 110°C. chemical structure-to-material property relat ionship. The organic mater ials within the leg 2 subst rate focus on higher aromatic content in the polymer structure for crack resistance. As an example, the solder mask for leg 2 has a Tg ~30 degrees higher than that of the solder mask in leg 1. Furthermore, the elastic modulus is ~2x higher and the coeff icient of thermal expansion (CTE) is 3x lower for the solder mask in leg 2 as compared to leg 1. A higher cross-link density created in the leg 2 material set can provide a path for a larger degree of thermo-mechanical integrity as compared to the lower Tg solder mask in leg 1. Literature repor ts that molecular weight and cross-link density cannot be ignored when comparing activation energies of different polymeric material sets [8]. Independent thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) using a decomposition kinetics technique to derive activation energy of the neat material associated w i t h  t h e  o r g a n i c  s u b s t r a t e  ( i . e . , solder mask and ABF dielectric) have conf i rmed claims in the l iterature. TGA is not repor ted in th is st udy, however, the analysis was based on ASTM designation: E1641-16 [9]. The overlaying context of the hypothesis being the lower the activation energy, the easier it  is  to t r igger a fai lu re mechanism as a function of temperature. Impact of act ivat ion energy on performance. When compared against the legacy activation energy of 0.7eV for sil icon, the empir ically-der ived ac t iva t ion  ene rg y for  t he  orga n ic material sets involved in legs 1 and 2 span a wide range of activation energies based on the chemical st ructure-to-mater ial proper ty relat ionship. For example, the activation energy for leg 1 as calculated by JMP was found to be ~1.5eV lower than the legacy value. On the  othe r  hand ,  t he  ac t ivat ion energy for leg 2 was found to be ~1.5eV higher than the legacy value. As stated prev iously,  there is  l i te rat u re that reports an erroneous calculation of 0.1eV at 60°C; what this means is that if the legacy value of 0.7eV increases +/-0.1eV at 60°C, this can increase or decrease the mean time to failure by a factor of more than 30. With the empirically-derived activation energies for legs 1 and 2 falling outside the aforementioned 0.1eV band (as reported in literature), a  c o m p a r i s o n  i n  t h e  n u m b e r  o f stress hours required to simulate an equivalence to a 100K POH environment at continuous use temperatures of 110°C was compared to the legacy 0.7eV value for silicon reliability. the activation energy for the thermal oxidat ive degradat ive failure mode was pursued using a FCBGA device manufactured with two different organic packaging material sets used in HVM. The FCBGA was tested through electrical failure at different HTSL conditions of 150°C, 160°C, and 175°C to trigger the temperature-accelerated failure mechanism and activation energy. Results were consistent with literature findings that the applicability of the legacy 0.7eV activation energy as a universal value is not prudent to obtain reliable lifetime predictions. In this study the empirically-derived activation energy was found to overlap the legacy value, where the spread was attributed to the chemical structure-t o -mate r ia l  p rope r t y  r e la t ion sh ip differences between leg 1 and leg 2 material sets. The difference in lifetime prediction between the legacy 0.7eV versus the empirically-obtained activation energy was a factor of 4. For zero defect applications like automotive, this factor disparity can be highly consequential and reinforces both the quantitative and qualitative need to correlate activation energy to the cor responding failure mode. Further investigation is warranted on the subject and perhaps at the JEDEC publ icat ion level  based on market trends for advanced flip-chip packaging p r o l i f e r a t i n g  w i t h i n  b u r g e o n i n g artif icial intelligence (AI), HPC, and automotive applications. Results show an order of magnitude difference in acceleration factor in legs 1 and 2 as compared to the baseline. Tables 2-4  illustrate the point, which is based on normalized values. Use and stress temperatures and POH are fixed variables in the relative comparison assessment of the impact of activation energy. Acceleration factor and simulated stress hours equivalent to 100K POH are the variables. Table 2 depicts the normalized values using the legacy 0.7eV as the baseline. Table 3 shows a normalized assessment of leg 1 with an empirically-derived activation energy ~1.5x lower than the legacy value. To quantify the impact of activation energy for leg 1, if the legacy 0.7eV value is used to simulate a thermal oxidation degradation failure mechanism for st ress hours, the simulated stress duration would be unreliable by 4.2x. Comparatively, results in Table 4 indicate that using the legacy 0.7eV value to simulate a thermal oxidation degradation failure mechanism for stress hours required at 150°C for leg 2 would be unreliable by 0.4x. The main takeaway is leg 2 with the higher activation energy provides a lower failure probability on the scale of several orders of parts per million (ppm) difference. Examples of failure probability differences are in Figures 8-9 using 6,000 hours at HTSL 150°C as the reference condition. Summary The d r iv ing force for th is  s t udy originated because of the scarcity of an empi r ical ly-obtained act ivat ion energy correlated to thermal oxidative degradat ion for organic packaging material sets subjected to long-term POH and continuous use temperatures. Ostensibly, the ubiquitous use of the legacy activation energy of 0.7eV for silicon reliability has been a panacea for failure modes (in general). Its usage has been highly questioned in the literature and shown to be unreliable leading to erroneous lifetime predictions. As a result, a more direct derivation of 3. M. Source: vSphere VMDirectPath I/O and Dynamic DirectPath I/O: Requirements for Platforms and Devices - Broadcom support portal

Cookies Settings vSphere VMDirectPath I/O and Dynamic DirectPath I/O: Requirements for Platforms and Devices search cancel Search vSphere VMDirectPath I/O and Dynamic DirectPath I/O: Requirements for Platforms and Devices book Article ID: 312208 calendar_today Updated On: 12-10-2025 Feedback Subscribe Products VMware vSphere ESXi Show More Show Less Issue/Introduction This article lists requirements and recommendations for hardware platforms and PCI devices in order to make them suitable for vSphere VMDirectPath I/O and Dynamic DirectPath I/O. Resolution About VMDirectPath I/O VMDirectPath I/O (PCI passthrough) enables direct assignment of hardware PCI Functions to virtual machines. This gives the virtual machine access to the PCI Functions with minimal intervention from the ESXi host, potentially improving performance. It is suitable for performance critical workloads such as graphics acceleration for virtual desktops, such as VMware View vDGA, and high data-rate networking such as those found in enterprise class telecommunications equipment. It works particularly well with PCI devices supporting SR-IOV technology, as each virtual function in the device can be assigned to a separate virtual machine. While VMDirectPath I/O can improve the performance of a virtual machine, enabling it makes several important features of vSphere unavailable to the virtual machine, such as Suspend and Resume, Snapshots, Fault Tolerance, and vMotion. How to use this document Confirm that the Server platform is listed as compatible in the Broadcom Compatibility Guide. Each certified Server vendor is expected to verify that their platform meets the requirements listed in the Platform Requirements and Recommendations for VMDirectPath I/O section. Confirm device is listed in the Broadcom Compatibility Guide and ensure Server vendor and device vendor supports the configuration by contacting the server vendor or checking server vendor's documentation or device vendor or checking device vendor's documentation. Confirm that the Guest OS release running in the Virtual Machine is compatible with the ESXi release in the Broadcom Compatibility Guide . And ensure a recommended/certified in-guest device driver is installed in the guest operating system. A driver for the device can be obtained either from the device vendor or guest operating system vendor. Confirm that the guest operating system vendor supports the configuration. Confirm that any optional VMware products and solutions are compatible with the configuration. Confirm that the vendors of third-party workload running in the VM support the configuration. Platform Requirements and Recommendations for VMDirectPath I/O IOMMU The platform must have an IOMMU for DMA remapping any PCI Function which is to be assigned for VMDirectPath I/O. The IOMMU's DMA re-mapping functionality is necessary in order for VMDirectPath I/O to work. DMA transactions sent by the passthrough PCI Function carry guest OS physical addresses which must be translated into host physical addresses by the IOMMU. PCI Express Access Control Services (ACS) DMA requests emitted by a passthrough PCI Function carry guest OS physical addresses rather than host OS physical addresses, so they cannot route correctly in the host's PCI bus hierarchy in the absence of ACS. With ACS, these requests are unconditionally routed to the platform's IOMMU for address translation. ACS support is required in the PCIe root ports and the PCIe switch downstream ports which are located above the passthrough PCI Function in the PCI bus hierarchy. ACS is also required if the passthrough PCI Function is part of a multi-function device and supports peer-to-peer transfers. Device Requirements and Recommendations PCI Function BARs PCI Function BARs must be aligned to 4KB addresses or multiples of it (For example: 8KB, 16KB, etc.) in the host machine's physical address space. If the PCI Function's BAR size is greater than or equal to 4KB, this requirement is implicitly satisfied. If the PCI Function's BAR size is less than 4KB, platform firmware must align the start of the BAR as stated above, and must ensure no other system resources are mapped within the 4KB page at which the BAR is mapped. Resizable BAR functionality is unsupported for PCI devices in passthrough mode. PCI Function BARs size requirements across product versions: ESXi 5.1 and 5.5 The maximum supported size for a single PCI BAR is 1GB. The combined size of all the BARs in the PCI Function must not exceed 3.75GB. The amount of BAR space consumed by other PCI devices in the virtual machine will further limit this, as the combined size of all PCI BARs in the virtual machine is 3.75GB or less. For ESXi 5.1 and 5.5, virtual machines that use legacy BIOS firmware, which maps BARs below the 4GB address boundary, the requirements arise from BAR alignment requirements and memory reservations by the BIOS. ESXi 6.0 If the virtual machine uses Legacy BIOS mode, the limits for ESXi 6.0 are identical to those in ESXi 5.5. To use more than 3.75GB of total BAR allocation within a virtual machine add this line to the virtual machine's vmx file to set the virtual machine BIOS to use UEFI: firmware="efi" To enable 64-bit Memory Mapped I/O (MMIO) add this line to the virtual machine vmx file: pciPassthru.use64bitMMIO="TRUE" In ESXi 6.0 Patch 4 and later the system can use more than 32GB, by specifying the size of the MMIO region as a power-of-two number of GB in the virtual machine's vmx file: For example: pciPassthru.64bitMMIOSizeGB = "128" Set the ESXi hosts BIOS to allow PCI mapping above 4GB and below 16TB. In UEFI BIOS mode, a virtual machines's total BAR allocation is limited to 32GB. ESXi 6.5.x, 6.7.x and 7.0.x If the virtual machine uses Legacy BIOS mode, the limits for ESXi 6.5.x, 6.7.x and 7.0.x are identical to those in ESXi 6.0. If the virtual machine uses UEFI BIOS mode, the ESXi 6.0 limitations apply with the exception that the 32GB limit has been removed and the new limit on total BAR allocation for a virtual machine is based on the hardware limits. For more information, see https://knowledge.broadcom.com/external/article/341298 . Ensure the virtual machine is using UEFI BIOS by looking in the .vmx file for these entries: firmware=“efi" To enable 64-bit Memory Mapped I/O (MMIO) add this line to the virtual machine vmx file: pciPassthru.use64bitMMIO="TRUE" To use more than 32GB, specify the size of the MMIO region as a power-of-two number of GB in the virtual machine's vmx file: For example: pciPassthru.64bitMMIOSizeGB = "128" PCI Function resets ESXi resets PCI Functions assigned for VMDirectPath I/O in order to provide isolation between the ESXi host and the virtual machine. This is done to ensure guest operating systems see a device with clean state during power-up or reboot. ESXi supports the use of several reset types for PCI Functions: Function Level Reset (FLR) Secondary Bus Reset Link Disable/Enable Device power state transition (D0 > D3hot > D0; non-standard reset method) The selected method is configurable via the passthru.map file in the ESXi host located at /etc/vmware/passthru.map. The FLR and Device power state transition reset types have function-level granularity, meaning that the reset can be applied on a single PCI Function without affecting other PCI Functions in the device or other devices in the same bus. Conversely, Secondary Bus Reset and Link Disable/Enable have bus-level granularity, meaning that the reset affects all PCI Functions on the same bus (For example, all PCI Functions in a multi-function PCIe device). Requirements and Recommendations: In order to guarantee isolation between the ESXi host and the virtual machine, the PCI Function must meet the following criteria after a PCI reset. These criteria are from the PCIe 3.0 specification, section 6.6.2: The Function must not give the appearance of an initialized adapter within the host or its external interfaces (For example, the Function must be quiesced and not issue any transactions or interrupts). The Function must not retain software readable state that potentially includes secret information associated with any preceding use of the function. Normal configuration should cause the Function to be usable by its drivers. VMware strongly recommends that PCI Functions support Function-Level-Reset (FLR). For reset methods with bus-level granularity, VMDirectPath I/O is only supported if all PCI Functions on the same bus are collectively assigned to the same virtual machine. ESXi 5.5 and 6.0 detect reset dependencies automatically and notify the user about them when a given PCI Function is assigned for VMDirectPath I/O to a virtual machine. ESXi 5.1 does not detect reset dependencies automatically. Such dependencies must be explicitly configured by the user through the passthru.map file, by setting the shareable attribute of the PCI Function to false. A PCI Function directly under a PCI Host Bridge must support FLR or D3Hot reset in order for it to be eligible for VMDirectPath I/O. Multi-Function PCI Devices It is strongly recommended that a multi-function PCI device not have functional-dependencies across its PCI Functions. If a functional-dependency exists between PCI Functions of a multi-function device, all dependent PCI Functions in the same device must be collectively assigned for VMDirectPath I/O to the same virtual machine. Such dependencies must be explicitly configured by the user via the passthru.map file, by setting the shareable attribute of the dependent PCI Functions to false. Peer-to-peer DMA Transactions As of ESXi 7.0 U2 and newer, peer-to-peer DMA transactions are supported with the following entries in the virtual machine vmx file: pciPassthru.allowP2P = true pciPassthru.relaxACSforP2P = true This will allow support for peer-to-peer DMA transactions to/from a PCI passthrough device in a VM PCI Functions behind legacy PCI Bridges VMware strongly recommends that PCI Functions assigned for VMDirectPath I/O be placed behind PCI Express root ports or switch downstream ports. VMware discourages VMDirectPath I/O assignment of PCI Functions behind conventional PCI bridges or PCIe-to-PCI/PCI-X bridges. PCI Functions behind PCIe to PCI/PCI-X bridges or PCI conventional bridges must be collectively assigned for VMDirectPath I/O to the same virtual machine. These bridges take ownership of PCI transactions sent by PCI Functions behind them by placing the bridge's PCI requester ID on the transactions. This forces the ESXi host to program IOMMU translations using the PCI Bridge's requester ID, implying that all PCI Functions behind the bridges must be placed in the same IOMMU domain and therefore be collectively assigned to the same virtual machine. PCI root complex integrated endpoints PCI passthrough of root-complex integrated endpoints (i.e., PCI Functions directly under a PCI host bridge) is supported for regular (non SR-IOV) PCI Functions. It is not supported for SR-IOV Virtual Functions at this time. PCI SR-IOV Devices PCI passthrough of SR-IOV virtual functions is supported, as long as the PCI platform and device requirements stated in this document are met. PCI passthrough of SR-IOV physical functions with the purpose of allowing the guest OS to enable VFs is not supported at this time. Specifically, ESXi does not currently support virtualizing the PF's SR-IOV Capability to the guest OS. PCI passthrough of SR-IOV physical functions for other purposes is allowed, as long as the PCI platform and device requirements stated in this document are met. PCI Functions that DMA to host reserved memory VMware strongly recommends that PCI Functions that are assigned for VMDirectPath I/O do not generate DMA read/writes to ESXi host memory marked as reserved in the platform's memory map (For example, BIOS E820 or UEFI GetMemoryMap()). PCI Functions that generate DMA read\writes to host reserved memory forces the ESXi host to create an IOMMU identity-map for the reserved memory ranges (since such DMAs carry host physical addresses rather than guest physical addresses). This creates addressing constraints for the ESXi host and the virtual machine as described below. If a PCI Function DMAs to the ESXi host reserved memory, the PCI Function and the reserved ranges it DMAs to must appear in the ACPI Reserved Memory Region Reporting structure (RMRR). ESXi's support for VMDirectPath I/O of PCI Functions that DMA to host reserved memory varies across product versions: ESXi 5.1 VMDirectPath I/O of PCI Functions that DMA to host reserved memory is not supported, unless a reset of the PCI Function causes it to stop DMAing to such memory. ESXi 5.5 (versions prior to ESX 5.5U3) VMDirectPath I/O of PCI Functions that DMA to host reserved memory is supported, subject to the following: The RMRR regions associated with the PCI Function must be placed in one of the following ranges, in order of preference: Outside of the VM's physical memory range (i.e., above the highest address configured for the VM's RAM). Below the 4GB address boundary, in the VM's PCI memory hole. The VM's .vmx file must be configured to map the VM's PCI memory hole such that it overlaps with the RMRR region (by setting configuration option “pciHole.start” and “pciHole.size”). The PCI memory hole must be in the address range 256MB -> 4GB. Explanation: the RMRR ranges must never overlap with the VM's physical memory range (except as described above), as otherwise ESXi would not be able to program IOMMU address translations correctly. ESXi resets PCI Functions assigned VMDirectPath I/O. If the platform depends on the PCI Function DMAing to the RMRR regions for correct operation, it must ensure that the PCI Function continues to do this after the reset by using a platform-specific mechanism. If this is not possible, then the PCI Function should not be assigned for VMDirectPath I/O. ESXi 5.5 (starting with 5.5U3), ESXi 6.x Starting with ESXi 5.5 U3, ESXi will automatically adjust the VM's physical memory map to prevent overlaps between host RMRR regions and the VM's RAM. However, the automatic adjustment only works if the following conditions are met. The RMRR regions associated with the PCI Function must be placed in one of the following ranges: Outside of the VM's physical memory range (i.e., above the highest address configured for the VM's RAM). Below the 4GB address boundary, in the range 256MB -> 4GB. In the VM's BIOS reserved memory (i.e., 640K - 1MB). ESXi resets PCI Functions assigned VMDirectPath I/O. If the platform depends on the PCI Function DMAing to the RMRR regions for correct operation, it must ensure that the PCI Function continues to do this after the reset by using a platform-specific mechanism. If this is not possible, then the PCI Function should not be assigned for VMDirectPath I/O. ESXi 7.0 ESXi 7.0 will automatically adjust the VM's physical memory map to prevent overlaps between host RMRR regions and the VM's RAM, using the same algorithm as ESXi 5.5U3 and above. However, when using Dynamic DirectPath I/O (available starting from ESXi 7.0), users can specify multiple valid vendor/device combinations and allow ESXi to choose the specific device based on what is available at the time of VM power-on. ESXi will try to automatically adjust the VM's physical memory map to accommodate all the allowable devices, but depending on where the devices place their RMRRs, this may not always be possible. If the automatic adjustment is unable to accommodate the selected device, the vmware.log file will show the following error: 2019-10-09T17:21:14.247Z| vmx| I125: PCIPassthru: Failed to register device 0000:21:00.0 error = 0x1c 2019-10-09T17:21:14.247Z| vmx| I125: Msg_Post: Error 2019-10-09T17:21:14.247Z| vmx| I125: [msg.pciPassthru.createAdapterFailedDeviceOverlap] Device 033:00.0 has an overlapping memory region with the virtual machine's memory. 2019-10-09T17:21:14.247Z| vmx| I125: ---------------------------------------- In that case, the user may need to reduce their selection to a single vendor/device combination or to a subset where all the RMRR regions fit within the VM's memory holes. Note that the above error can also occur if the conditions for the automatic adjustment algorithm are not met. PCI Interrupts VMDirectPath I/O supports PCI Functions that use MSI-X, MSI, or legacy (i.e., INTx) PCI interrupts. VMware recommends that PCI Functions used for VMDirectPath I/O support MSI or MSI-X interrupts. Explanation: PCI Functions using INTx may share the interrupt line with other PCI Functions in the system. This can delay the servicing of the interrupt for each PCI Function sharing the interrupt. The delay is exacerbated if one or more of the PCI Functions sharing the INTx is assigned for VMDirectPath I/O. PCI Errors ESXi does not support virtualization of PCI errors for VMDirectPath I/O (i.e., PCI Errors reported by a PCI Function assigned for VMDirectPath I/O are always handled by ESXi and never presented to the guest OS). Due to platform constraints on current generation servers, PCI uncorrectable errors typically cause an NMI to be delivered to the processors, from which ESXi cannot currently recover. As a result, VMware requires that PCI Functions that may be assigned for VMDirectPath I/O be designed to avoid emitting PCI uncorrectable errors under normal operating conditions. PCI Functions with Expansion ROM ESXi supports VMDirectPath I/O of PCI Functions with Expansion ROM (e.g., the Expansion ROM code may contain device-specific initialization and/or code that allows a VM to boot from the passthrough PCI Function). The Expansion ROM is executed by the VM's firmware whenever the VM is powered-on or restarted. A hot-reset of the device should place the device in a state where the Expansion ROM can be re-executed. Execution of Expansion ROM code by the VM's firmware is disabled by default but can be enabled as follows: Setting the ESXi boot option 'pcipSaveOPROM=TRUE'. Configuring the VM's .vmx file with parameter “%s.opromEnabled=TRUE” (e.g., pciPassthru0.opromEnabled=TRUE). Future Implementation Note: Though currently not supported, future versions of ESXi may support re-assigning a VMDirectPath I/O PCI Function back to the ESXi VMkernel at run-time (i.e., without having to power-down the ESXi host). For PCI Functions with an Expansion ROM, this operation won't be supported since the PCI Function is reset during this re-assignment and the Expansion ROM won't be executed by ESXi (i.e., such code is only executed by the host machine's BIOS/UEFI firmware during boot time). PCI Power Management ESXi does not support virtualization of PCI Power Management Events (PME) for VMDirectPath I/O (i.e., PME messages generated by VMDirectPath I/O PCI Functions are currently ignored by ESXi and do not cause a notification to the guest OS). However, the VM's BIOS does by default grant control of PCIe Native Power Management Events to guest OSes that request so via the APCI _OSC method. This may cause the guest OS to enable PMEs in the passthrough PCI Function, which could lead to malfunction for PCI Functions that rely on the generation of PMEs for correct operation (e.g., a PCI NIC where its driver may place it in a low power state during periods of low traffic, where the driver relies on the NIC generating a PME to place it back into a normal power state). This situation may be prevented by configuring the VM's BIOS from granting control of PCIe Native Power Management Events to the guest OS. This is done via the .vmx configuration option 'acpi.osc.pcie', which overrides the _OSC Control Field, Return Value. Refer to the ACPI specification for the format of the _OSC Control Field, Return value. General Testing Recommendations To increase the likelihood of both success and overall system stability with the VMDirectPath I/O implementation, VMware strongly encourages that OEM partners perform rigorous testing on the specific platforms they intend to use VMDirectPath I/O with, to ensure the requirements stated in this document are met. This testing should account for the common power-on, restart, and functional test use-cases within the VM, but it must also consider more corner-case testing to attempt to fully validate the platform. Such corner cases primarily include forced shutdown (or crashing) of the VM, or forced shutdown of the ESXi host itself while the VM is running. Additional Information For platform requirements, see the Broadcom Compatibility Guide and select VM Direct Path IO under Features.
